    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="b7d8911a4aea472a85005c4effe59048">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '9c6f8727-9ca8-4605-89be-f024f856a8bd'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d25777443" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium metal battery is considered as one of the most promising energy storage/conversion devices due to their high energy density, and abundant sodium reserves. However, its development is hampered by the limited metallic utilization and detrimental sodium dendrite growth ascribed to the unstable, and fragile solid electrolyte interphase (SEI). Here, the high stable and modulus SEI is constructed with a component of a thin and dense layer of high Na-ion conductive Na<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>3</sub>. It is in situ formed by the reaction between sodium anode and fish-skin structure separator, which exhibits a 3D porous polyvinylidene fluoride-based framework embedded with thin and robust vermiculite sheets. The robust interphase cannot only suppress sodium dendrite growth but also enable high energy density with small interphase-consumed Na ions in anode-less batteries. Accordingly, the as-assembled NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>//Na cell shows 83% capacity retention after 5000 cycles at a rate of 5&nbsp;°C. Moreover, the proof-of-concept pouch cells deliver an energy density of 205&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (based on the total mass of the battery), and high safety (not catching fire after punctured) under anode-less condition (with a low negative/positive capacity ratio of ≈2.1).</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202407783-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202407783-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The use of renewable and clean energy, such as wind and solar power, integrated with rechargeable batteries to regulate energy output has offered a promising way to cope with climatic change.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202407783-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202407783-bib-0001_R_d25777427e478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nature">1</a>-<a href="#adfm202407783-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202407783-bib-0004_R_d25777427e478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Rev.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Owing to their high energy density and long cycling life, Li-ion batteries have become one of the most cost-effective options for sustainable energy storage and conversion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202407783-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202407783-bib-0005_R_d25777427e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater.">5</a>-<a href="#adfm202407783-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202407783-bib-0007_R_d25777427e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Chem. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the low abundance of Li in the Earth's crust (17&nbsp;ppm) makes it incapable of worldwide smart grids and large-scale energy storage systems.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202407783-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202407783-bib-0006_R_d25777427e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Chem. Rev.">6</a>, <a href="#adfm202407783-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202407783-bib-0008_R_d25777427e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energ. Environ. Sci.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> With physicochemical properties similar to Li, Na has abundant reserves and is evenly distributed, making Na-based batteries important candidates for large-scale energy storage systems.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202407783-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202407783-bib-0009_R_d25777427e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Rev.">9</a>-<a href="#adfm202407783-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202407783-bib-0012_R_d25777427e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Energy.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In comparison with Na-ion batteries, Na metal batteries (NMBs) that use metallic Na anodes with a high theoretical specific capacity (1166&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>), and low redox potential (2.71&nbsp;V VS standard hydrogen electrode) exhibit outstanding advantages in energy density.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202407783-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202407783-bib-0013_R_d25777427e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Chem">13</a>-<a href="#adfm202407783-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202407783-bib-0018_R_d25777427e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Central Sci">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> High-temperature Na-S batteries have been used worldwide as energy storage systems. However, when working at room temperature, the major barrier for the application of Na metal anode in NMBs is the unstable and fragile SEI, which not only leads to continuous consumption of electrolytes and low Coulombic efficiencies of Na stripping/plating but also intensifies the fatal growth of dendrites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202407783-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202407783-bib-0018_R_d25777427e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Central Sci">18</a>-<a href="#adfm202407783-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202407783-bib-0020_R_d25777427e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To date, considerable focus has been placed on constructing the desirable SEI for suppressing Na dendrite growth and improving the Coulombic efficiencies to obtain a long cycle life, and high energy density in NMBs by engineering liquid electrolytes,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202407783-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202407783-bib-0018_R_d25777427e514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Central Sci">18</a>-<a href="#adfm202407783-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202407783-bib-0027_R_d25777427e514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> building ex situ artificial SEI protection layers,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202407783-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202407783-bib-0028_R_d25777427e519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Mater.">28</a>-<a href="#adfm202407783-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202407783-bib-0032_R_d25777427e519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Energy Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and optimizing solid-state electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202407783-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202407783-bib-0033_R_d25777427e524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Nat. Nanotech.">33</a>-<a href="#adfm202407783-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202407783-bib-0036_R_d25777427e524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Energy Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Wan et&nbsp;al., developed a binary electrolyte to form NaF-rich SEI, realizing the anode-free NMBs with 750 charge–discharge cycles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202407783-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202407783-bib-0024_R_d25777427e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Wang et&nbsp;al., built a fluorinated porous framework on Na metal with good sodiophilicity and low interphase-consumed Na<sup>+</sup>, enabling 400 stable cycles under anode-less conditions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202407783-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202407783-bib-0032_R_d25777427e537" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Energy Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Lu et&nbsp;al., constructed beta-alumina solid-state electrolyte forming β-NaAlO<sub>2</sub>-rich solid–electrolyte interphase with a capacity retention of 73% over 500 cycles and a high Coulombic efficiency of &gt;99.99%.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202407783-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202407783-bib-0034_R_d25777427e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Joule">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although the components and structure of SEI have been optimized to achieve highly reversible Na stripping/plating, a robust, thin, and uniform SEI with negligible Na<sup>+</sup> depletion, and gaseous byproducts generated during SEI formation remains an important challenge and unresolved issue.</p>
         
         <p>In this study, we develop a robust and highly conductive SEI on the Na metal anode by rationally designing a fish skin-like bilayer separator comprising a 3D porous polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF)-based framework embedded with a thin and robust vermiculite sheet (VS) layer via non-solvent-induced phase separation. The VS layer enables the in situ formation of a thin and dense Na<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>3</sub> dominant SEI with negligible interphase-consumed Na<sup>+</sup>. The high modulus VS layer and dense SEI effectively suppressed sodium dendrite growth. Meanwhile, the fish skin structure bi-layer separator demonstrates the excellent thermal stability of NMBs, in which the VS layer helps maintain the dimensional stability of the separator and the PVDF framework can shut down the Na-ion diffusion channels by blocking the micro-holes under abuse. Accordingly, the Na//Na symmetric cell exhibited a remarkable Coulombic efficiency of ≈99.5% for 1100 h. The NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>//Na cell demonstrated a stable cycle life of up to 5000 cycles at a current density of 5&nbsp;°C. Furthermore, owing to this high modulus and dense SEI, the as-prepared pouch cell showed impressive capacity retention of 85% after 500 cycles. It also delivers a high energy density of 205&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (based on the total mass of the battery) and high safety (not catching fire after being punctured) under anode-less conditions.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202407783-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202407783-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202407783-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202407783-sec-0030-title">2.1 Morphology and Physical Electrochemical Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>The fish skin-like bilayer separator was achieved by mixing PVDF and VS in the dimethyl formamide and covering them on the glass panel (named PVDF-VS separator). The VS was stacked layer-to-layer with the formation of a self-assembly vermiculite sheet-rich dense layer by a non-solvent-induced phase separation process when the glass panel was soaked in water (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0001">1</a>). The high modulus VS layer was overlaid across the soft PVDF framework, like fish scales, contributing to the high strength and toughness of the PVDF-VS separator. Separators with different mass ratios of PVDF and VS were prepared to determine the impact of the PVDF and VS content on the morphology and physical electrochemical properties of the PVDF-VS. The mass ratios of VS/PVDF were set as 0%, 10%, 20%, 30%, and 40% and were denoted as S0, S1, S2, S3, and S4, respectively (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0002">2a</a>, as the content of VS increased, the typical X-ray diffraction peak of VS intensified. The Raman spectra show the same shift (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0002">2b</a>). In addition, the β-phase PVDF tends to dominate with the increase of VS, which is also confirmed by the Fourier transformation infrared spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0002">2c</a>). Specifically, the all-trans conformation (TTTT) of β-phase PVDF exhibits higher polarity than that of α phase PVDF consisting of alternate trans and gauche conformations (TGTG).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202407783-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202407783-bib-0037_R_d25777427e607" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Prog.Polym. Sci.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) shows that the Si2p becomes more pronounced at higher VS content (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0002">2d</a>). The VS-free separator (S0) exhibits a uniform inter-connective honeycombed porous structure with pore sizes of ≈3&nbsp;µm (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). For the S1 separator, most of the VSs are distributed along one side of the separator, with several VSs horizontally laid on the surface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0002">2e</a>). As the VS content increases, more VSs aggregate on the separator surface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0002">2f</a>). In particular, S3 exhibits a half-through and half-pore spongy structure, in which the VS spreads one after another on one side, similar to a fish scale (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0002">2g,i</a>). When the mass ratios of VS/PVDF reached 40%, the surfaces of S4 were shaded by numerous VSs and the VSs penetrated the PVDF-based framework (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0002">2h</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202407783-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2796ebe2-f32c-4603-88eb-26461b15a0c5/adfm202407783-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/2796ebe2-f32c-4603-88eb-26461b15a0c5/adfm202407783-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/788d02a3-13ee-48e0-89c9-ef6f4cfe862a/adfm202407783-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2796ebe2-f32c-4603-88eb-26461b15a0c5/adfm202407783-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202407783-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202407783" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The fabrication diagram of the double-layer PVDF-based composite embedded with vermiculite sheet.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202407783-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ca63a201-bab1-4237-ab38-1ff6b1ade7a1/adfm202407783-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/ca63a201-bab1-4237-ab38-1ff6b1ade7a1/adfm202407783-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8449d964-92b7-44ca-85fd-ba45a8c37634/adfm202407783-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ca63a201-bab1-4237-ab38-1ff6b1ade7a1/adfm202407783-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202407783-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202407783" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Preparation and structural characterization of the fish-skin-like bilayer separator. a) XRD, b) Raman spectra, c) FT-IR and d) XPS results of the as-prepared S0, S1, S2, S3, and S4 separators, respectively. SEM images from front view of e) S1, f) S2, g) S3, and h) S4 separators, respectively (inserts are SEM images from side view). i) SEM image and j) corresponding EDS mapping of C, F, O, and Si elements of the top surface of S3 separator from the VS side. k) AFM and l) 3D AFM image and Young's modulus mapping of the top surface of S3 separator from the VS side.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The morphology and composition of separators significantly affect their physicochemical characteristics. Tensile strength measurements demonstrate that S0 exhibits the highest elongation at break (350%) and S3 shows the highest tensile strength (12&nbsp;MPa). In comparison, S4 delivers the lowest elongation at break (50%) and tensile strength (5&nbsp;MPa), which are too low to satisfy application requirements (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). Owing to the porous structure, all separators are endowed with a high electrolyte uptake, with S3 exhibiting the highest uptake (430%, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Owing to the hydrophilicity, hydrophilic of VS, the water contact angle gradually decreases from 114.0° to 71.3° with an increase in VS content (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). Therefore, the high porosity, sufficient electrolyte uptake, and good affinity between electrolyte and separator in the S3 film are beneficial to fast Na<sup>+</sup> migration, resulting in the ion conductivity of ≈1.75 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information) and the Na-ion transference number of S3 (0.53), which is higher than that of commercial separator (CS) (0.42) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, the S3 separator shows a high oxidation resistance of 5.3&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na, which adapts to most high-voltage cathodes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). Importantly, due to the high content of VS, S3 film reveals high thermal dimensional stability as there is no obvious shrinkage even at 200&nbsp;°C (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>Because the S3 separator exhibits the best compromise in terms of mechanical strength, ionic conductivity, electrochemical sable window, and thermal stability, we further conducted a deep structural analysis of the S3 film. Energy-dispersive X-ray (EDX) elemental mapping analysis from the front view shows that Si and O elements of the VS are homogenously dispersed throughout the PVDF matrix (F and C) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0002">2i,j</a>). The cross-sectional SEM reveals that the holes ≈20&nbsp;µm length are arranged in a honeycomb lattice penetrated through half of the film (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10a</a>, Supporting Information). The other half shows a sponge structure, with VS covering the top surface of the S3 film. The EDX mapping images also display the semi-through-blind holes and evenly distributed C and F elements of the PVDF matrix throughout the film (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10b</a>, Supporting Information). In comparison, the O and Si elements belonging to VS are distributed almost entirely on the top surface of the S3 film. To further test the distribution of VS on the S3 surface, an atomic force microscope (AFM) was used to characterize the surface roughness. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0002">2k,l</a>, the difference in height within the entire range is no more than 500&nbsp;nm, indicating the smooth, uniform surface of S3. The Young's modulus of the VS-covered surface is as high as 8&nbsp;GPa, which contributes to the inhibition of Na dendrite growth (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0002">2l</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). Overall, the S3 separator is an ideal candidate for a dendrite-free sodium metal battery.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202407783-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202407783-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical performance of a sodium metal battery with the S3 separator was systematically analyzed with S0, S1, S2, S4, and CS as a comparison. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0003">3a</a> compares the long-cycle stability of Na symmetrical cells cycling at a current density of 0.5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> for 1 h. The cell using CS exhibits a relatively high overpotential (80&nbsp;mV) and short cycling lifespan (≈300&nbsp;h). The symmetric Na cell using the S0 separator only delivers a cycling life of only 210&nbsp;h under high overpotential (100&nbsp;mV). After 360&nbsp;h cycling, the cell with the S4 separator suffers from a short circuit. The symmetric Na cells using S1 and S2 separators show increased voltage polarization after 300&nbsp;h. Encouragingly, the symmetric Na cell using the S3 separator shows a much lower overpotential (≈50&nbsp;mV), flatter voltage profile, and longer plating/stripping life (1100&nbsp;h) than other five separators. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0003">3b</a> shows the rate behaviors of the symmetric cell using an S3 separator at a current density ranging from 0.5 to 5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with a duration of 1&nbsp;h. The symmetric cell exhibits small polarization voltages of 50, 150, 180, 210, and 320&nbsp;mV at the current densities of 0.5, 1, 2, 4, and 5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, respectively. Na||S3||Cu asymmetric cells were assembled to evaluate the reversibility of the Na metal anodes. The initial coulombic efficiency (CE) of the cell is 94.5%. After the first five cycles, the CE is stable at ≈99.3% with slight fluctuation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0003">3c,d</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202407783-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ebf67893-3887-4d11-9a53-f86db519a633/adfm202407783-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/ebf67893-3887-4d11-9a53-f86db519a633/adfm202407783-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/72e99498-2160-4929-ba50-5aaf181f8b3e/adfm202407783-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ebf67893-3887-4d11-9a53-f86db519a633/adfm202407783-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202407783-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202407783" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performances of Na metal anodes and NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>//Na coin cells. a) Cycling performances of Na||Na symmetric cells with CS, S0, S1, S2, S3, and S4 separators (0.5, 0.5 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>), respectively. b) Rate performance of the symmetric cells. c)The voltage-capacity profiles and d) CE in Na||Cu asymmetric cells using S3 separator with the current density of 0.5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and areal capacity of 0.5 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. e) Discharge–charge profiles of NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>//Na coin cells at 0.2 C (1 C = 140&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>). (f) Rate capability and (g) corresponding voltage–capacity profiles. (h) Cycling stability of NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>//Na batteries.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The representative NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>//Na coin cells using Na metal as a counter electrode (with a diameter of 1.4&nbsp;cm and thickness of 1&nbsp;mm) with the cathode mass loading of 5&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup> in a lean electrolyte state (≈20&nbsp;µL) were assembled to evaluate the electrochemical performance of the S3 separator in full cells. The cyclic voltammetry of a NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>//Na cell with the S3 separator shows two typical oxidation and three reduction peaks, and after the initial cycle, the followed four cycles are almost overlapped, which suggests the good reversibility of the NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>//Na cell (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0003">3e</a> shows the typical galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles (within 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V) of the full cell at 0.2 C. The initial charge capacity is ≈151.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and the initial discharge capacity is 138.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to an initial CE of ≈91%. The profiles remain highly consistent at 0.2 C, proving the highly reversible Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/desertion. The coin cells also exhibit highly reversible capacities of 138.2, 136.7, 131.1, 112.5, 97.6, and 46.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at increasing rates from 0.2 to 20 C, suggesting the ideal rate capability (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0003">3f,g</a>). By contrast, the full cells with S0 and CS separators decay quickly, exhibiting very low capacity (5 and 0.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively) at 20 C. The long cycle performance of NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>//Na cell using an S3 separator was also measured. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0003">3h</a>, the full cell displays remarkable capacity retention of 83% after 5000 cycles at 5 C. At a low rate of 0.5 C, it also retains ≈98% capacity retention after 100 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). In contrast, the cells using S0 and CS separators show an inferior cycling performance of no more than 500 cycles at 5 C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). The battery with an S1 separator shows ≈60% capacity retention after 1000 cycles. In addition, the batteries with S2 and S4 separators deliver ≈73% and 79% capacity retention after 1000 cycles, respectively. The battery with S3 separator maintains the highest capacity retention.</p>
            
            <p>To figure out the long cycle stability of NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>//Na cell with S3 separator, the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS), XRD and SEM tests of cycled NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> were conducted. The cell using the S3 separator shows lower interfacial resistance than those using the CS and S0 separator (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). After 200 cycles under a 5 C/5 C charge/discharge rate, the interfacial resistance of cells using the S3 separator exhibits a slight increase, whereas the interfacial resistances for cells using CS and S0 separators are greatly increased. The structural evolution of the cathode after 200 cycles with different separators was also investigated. The characteristic peaks of NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> with S3 separator are preserved even after 200 cycles, which is in contrast against the cathodes with CS and S0 that lose a majority of the layered signals after 200 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). We further characterized the morphological evolution of the cathode after cycling at the grain level by means of SEM. After 200 cycles, various wide cracks and ruptures appear in NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> particles using CS and S0 separators but none appears for those using the S3 separator, which kept a robust crystal morphology (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information). The contrast performance indicates that the S3 separator with VS can help form dense and stable SEI on Na metals, which should be beneficial to the good structure and morphology stability of NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode. Additionally, the NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>//Na cell using an S3 separator shows good cycle stability at elevated temperatures. The NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>//Na cell retains 77% capacity retention at 10 C under 60&nbsp;°C after 1000 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information). Not only NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, but the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode with S3 separator also demonstrates long cycle stability up to 1800 cycles at 10 C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202407783-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202407783-sec-0050-title">2.3 Interphase Formation on Na Metal Anode</h3>
            
            <p>Owing to the high chemical reactivity of Na metal, some of the inorganic/organic molecules on the surface were reduced to form an SEI film during the charge/discharge process. As mentioned above, in addition to the merits of the bulk characteristics of the separator, the SEI formed on the surface of Na metal is crucial for the long-term stability of NMBs. Therefore, the Na metal anodes after 100 cycles of NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>//Na full cells with S3 separator were analyzed by various technologies. The XRD of sodium metal after 100 cycles using the S3 separator shows the Na<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>3</sub> crystal phase (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information). From the transmission electron microscope analyses, it can be observed that these form smooth SEI (≈30&nbsp;nm) on the Na metal, and distributes many dense nanoparticles on one side of the SEI with the lattice corresponding to the (200) plane of Na<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>3</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information). The XPS results also show that inorganic components of <span class="icomoon"></span>CO<sub>3</sub>, <span class="icomoon"></span>F, <span class="icomoon"></span>SiO<sub>3</sub> and organic compound C─C, C─O and NaCO<sub>2</sub>R are detected in the sodium metal surface (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0004">4a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information). In particular, the appearance of Na<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>3</sub> suggests the reactions between VS on the S3 separator surface and Na metal. After etching this surface by Ar<sup>+</sup>, Si, F signal, as well as C─C, C─O and NaCO<sub>2</sub>R are decreased while the peak intensity of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> is still marked, implying that the SEI structure comprises NaF, Na<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>3</sub>, organic compound C─C, C─O, and NaCO<sub>2</sub>R rich in the outer layer and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> rich in the inner layer (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0004">4b</a>). The time-of-flight secondary ion mass spectrometry (TOF−SIMs) was performed to evaluate the composition of SEI. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0004">4c</a>, numerous inorganic species (Na<sup>+</sup>, SiO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>, F<sup>−</sup>, CO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>) can be detected, which are consistent with the XPS results. The normalized depth profiles of the F<sup>−</sup> and SiO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> fragments indicate a surface-concentrated distribution of total fluorides and silicates at the surface of Na metal. The corresponding 3D reconstruction images of sputtered volume (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0004">4d</a>) indicate that inorganic species are uniform and dominant in the SEI. The dense and uniform inorganic NaF and Na<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>3</sub> can protect Na metal from the continuous reaction between the Na anode and the electrolyte, resulting in highly reversible Na plating/stripping with high initial CE. Owing to the formation of NaF and Na<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>3</sub>, the SEI formed by the reaction between Na metal and VS delivers a high Young's modulus (2.2&nbsp;GPa) and low surface roughness (≈500&nbsp;nm) as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0004">4e,f</a>. As a result, the cycled Na metal using the S3 separator presents a flat and uniform morphology with uniform distributions of F, Si, and O elements (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0004">4g</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information). By contrast, numerous Na needles are observed on the surface of Na metal using CS (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0004">4h</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202407783-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/350425fe-fc9a-41b1-9f85-57fc19b66d5a/adfm202407783-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/350425fe-fc9a-41b1-9f85-57fc19b66d5a/adfm202407783-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/07a07e42-3c22-40ed-81ad-5511bef7afbb/adfm202407783-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/350425fe-fc9a-41b1-9f85-57fc19b66d5a/adfm202407783-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202407783-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202407783" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Morphology and composition of SEI films formed on Na metal using S3 separator. Ex situ XPS a) Si 2p and b) F 1s spectra collected before and after Ar<sup>+</sup> etching with different time. c) TOF−SIMs normalized depth profiles of the CO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>, Na<sup>+</sup>, SiO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> and F<sup>−</sup> fragments. d) 3D reconstruction images of sputtered volume for the Na<sup>+</sup>, SiO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>, F<sup>−</sup> and CO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> fragments. e) 3D AFM image, Young's modulus mapping and f) DMT modulus distribution of SEI. SEM images of SEIs formed on Na metal anodes using g) CS and h) S3 separators, respectively. Na ion diffusion pathways of i) Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and j) Na<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>3</sub>, (k) as well as barriers. Scheme of the evolution of SEI during Na striping/plating using (l) CS and (m) S3 separator. The blue particles represent inorganic components in SEI and the yellow layer represents VS layer of S3 separator. The gray part is Na metal and Na deposition.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To further reveal the influence of Na<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>3</sub> on the Na ion transport kinetics, theoretical calculations were carried performed to simulate the Na<sup>+</sup> transport pathways and barriers by comparison with Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>. The adsorption behavior of the Na adatoms on the crystal surfaces of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>3</sub> were calculated to describe the initial and end states of the diffusion path. The low surface free energy Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (010) and Na<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>3</sub> (001) were selected as the diffusion interface. The adsorption energy at different sites of Na adatoms flowing in different directions was investigated to determine the optimal diffusion path for Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>3</sub>, respectively. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0004">4i</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25a</a> (Supporting Information), the bridge sites between Na top and O top atoms are the preferred adsorption position for Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>. The transition state of Na<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>3</sub> is the adsorption of Na atoms at the bridge sites of Na and Si top atoms (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0004">4j</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25b</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a>, Supporting Information). Consequently, Na atoms exhibit a migration energy of 0.3&nbsp;eV to diffuse on Na<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>3</sub> (001). By contrast, Na atoms must overcome a larger energy barrier (0.6&nbsp;eV) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0004">4k</a>) to be removed from Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>. Therefore, the Na<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>3</sub> SEI acts as a fast conductor to facilitate the Na-ion transport, resulting in low polarization of the Na plating/stripping process. The routine Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>-riched SEI formed using a CS separator exhibits low mechanical stability and limited Na-ion transport kinetics. The low mechanical-stability SEI cannot maintain structural integrity owing to the huge volume change during Na plating/stripping, which induces fracture and restructuring of the SEI (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0004">4l</a>). Meanwhile, the low Na ion diffusion through the Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>-riched SEI leads to sluggish and heterogeneous Na growth, which further induces Na dendrite growth. When using the S3 separator, a dense Na<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>3</sub>-rich SEI generated through the in-situ reaction between VS and Na metal utilizes the high modulus that can tolerate the volume change and avoid cracking of the SEI (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0004">4m</a>). This utilization reduced the consumption of active Na<sup>+</sup> and electrolytes with a high CE. The fast Na-ion transport kinetics facilitate uniform deposition and simultaneously suppress Na dendrite growth.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202407783-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202407783-sec-0060-title">2.4 Building Anode-Less NMBs</h3>
            
            <p>The ultralong cycling stability of the S3 separator in the coin cell triggered our interest in evaluating its electrochemical performance at the pouch-cell level. NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>//Na pouch cells with a capacity of 1.5 Ah were assembled with a low negative/positive (N/P) ratio (2:1). The battery contains nine positive plates (5&nbsp;cm × 8&nbsp;cm) and ten negative plates. The active material area loading of the cathode was ≈30&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup> and the Na metal was loaded on Cu foam by melt impregnation with Na metal area loading of ≈7.2&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup> (details in Experimental Section, Supporting Information). The pouch cell using the S3 separator delivers an initial specific energy of 205&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2 C, based on the total weight of the pouch cell. Moreover, a stable capacity retention of 85% is achieved at 1 C after 500 cycles (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0005">5a</a>). The nail penetration test is regarded as the most demanding safety test for NMBs, particularly for NMBs with high energy density. Nail penetration tests of the 1.5 Ah pouch cells assembled with CS and S3 separators were conducted to investigate their safety performance. The cell assembled with the CS separator immediately broke out into a violent flame when the steel nail penetrated the cell (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0005">5b,c</a>; Video <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). However, no visible thermal runaway was observed during the nail penetration test of the cell assembled with an S3 separator (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0005">5d,e</a>; Video <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The cell can still retain a high voltage of 3.57&nbsp;V and a low internal resistance of 33.5 mΩ, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0005">5f</a>. The excellent thermal stability of NMBs can be ascribed to the stable SEI and high thermal dimensional stability of the S3 separator.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202407783-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ab070113-0144-49cd-b539-16d179cbfd12/adfm202407783-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/ab070113-0144-49cd-b539-16d179cbfd12/adfm202407783-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b2f50722-0c0a-4ae8-ba28-1484ebc6d5f2/adfm202407783-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ab070113-0144-49cd-b539-16d179cbfd12/adfm202407783-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202407783-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202407783" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performances of anode-less NMB pouch cell. a) Cycling performance of the pouch cell at 1 C (after five cycle's formation at 0.2 C, 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V). Photographs of (b) before and (c) after the nail penetration test of the batteries assembled with CS. Photographs of (d) before and (e) after the nail penetration test of the batteries assembled with S3 separator. f) Voltage and internal resistance of the cell assembled with S3 separator after testing. g) Schematic illustration for Na ion diffusion shutdown mechanism of S3 separator to prevent thermal runaway.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Although the mechanism of thermal runaway of NMBs is still controversial, it is important to suppress thermal runaway by preventing heat accumulation of up to 120&nbsp;°C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202407783-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202407783-bib-0038_R_d25777427e1316" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The accumulation of the heat released during the battery operation or under abuse, such as over-charge, short circuit, puncture, and so on, leads to the decomposition and regeneration of the solid electrolyte interphase. It will then drive the temperature the increase quickly due to the reaction between the high reactivity of fresh Na metal and electrolyte. The thermostability of the formed SEI on the surface of Na metal by using CS and S3 separators was probed by the in situ temperature change SEM. With the temperature rising to 80<sup>&nbsp;°</sup>C, there were obvious changes in the Na metal (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a>, Supporting Information). However, there is an obvious landslide on the surface of Na metal using CS with a temperature of up to 120<sup>&nbsp;°</sup>C due to the Na metal melt. In addition, there are many Na gushed out through the cracks on the surfaces. The piping fresh Na metal will react with the electrolyte generating plenty of heat. The heat leads to the shrinkage of the polyolefin separator, which consequently leads to a sharp rise in the cell temperature due to the internal short-circuit. At last, the thermal runaway occurs as happened in Video <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). Fortunately, the SEI on the surface of Na metal formed by using the S3 separator shows much higher thermostability than that using CS. Even at 120<sup>&nbsp;°</sup>C, the SEI on the surface of Na metal still holds together. No obvious crack on the surface can be observed although there is a landslide due to the Na metal melt. What's more, the dimensional stable S3 separator can prevent the internal short-circuit and the micro holes of the S3 separator can be blocked with unchanged overall dimensions (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a>, Supporting Information), which can effectively shut down the Na ion diffusion channels preventing further thermal runaway (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202407783-fig-0005">5g</a>). Owing to the thermostable SEI and dimensional stable S3 separator, the thermal runaway of NMBs under abuse can be effectively suppressed.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202407783-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202407783-sec-0070-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In this study, we develop a PVDF-VS composite separator with a fish skin-like bilayer structure for the long lifespan of NMBs, which benefits from a thin Na<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>3</sub>-rich SEI that was in situ formed by reactions between VS and Na metal. The SEI is endowed with a high Young's modulus, enhanced Na ion transport capability and durable interfacial stability, not only realizing rapid and reversible Na deposition but also maintaining the structural integrity without dendrite formation. Moreover, the fish skin-structured bilayer separator, which exhibits ideal dimensional stability supported by the VS layer, contributes to the excellent thermostability of NMBs under abuse. The NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>//Na cells exhibit a long cycle life of up to 5000 cycles. The pouch cells deliver a high energy of 205&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> and maintain 85% capacity retention after 500 cycles. Moreover, it exhibits high safety without causing fire after puncturing. This separator-induced protective interphase layer strategy opens a new avenue for the development of high-energy and long-life NMBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202407783-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202407783-sec-0080-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202407783-sec-0090">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202407783-sec-0100">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202407783-sec-0110">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202407783-sec-0110-title"> Materials</h4>
                  
                  <p>Polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF, AR) used in this work was supplied by Juhua Co, China, and dimethylacetamide (DMAc, AR), vermiculite, sodium chloride, lithium chloride were all supplied by Aladdin Industrial Co. Commercial separator (CS) of polypropylene with a thickness of 25&nbsp;µm was purchased from Meifeng (Hefei) Chemical Inc. The electrolyte employed was NaPF6 solution (concentration: 1.0 mol⋅L−1), which was provided in a mixture of ethylene carbonate (EC)/dimethyl carbonate (DMC)/ethyl methyl carbonate (EMC) (1:1:1, in weight ratio).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202407783-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202407783-sec-0120-title"> Preparation of PVDF/VS Composite Films</h4>
                  
                  <p>The peeling of VS was carried out in three steps. First, 2&nbsp;g vermiculite was weighed and soaked in 100&nbsp;mL H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> for 24&nbsp;h. Then, it was kept in a muffle furnace at 800&nbsp;°C for 1&nbsp;h to make thermal expansion, and excess H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> was removed. Subsequently, the obtained expanded vermiculite was fully ground, and stirred in a saturated NaCl solution at 110&nbsp;°C for 24&nbsp;h. Certain amounts of PVDF, DMAc and VS were added simultaneously into a flask equipped with a mechanical stirrer. The obtained mixture was subsequently heated to 50&nbsp;°C and stirred until a homogeneous solution formed. After that, the solution was kept at 50&nbsp;°C for at least 30&nbsp;min to remove bubbles. An appropriate amount of the homogeneous solution was cast on a glass plate, and film samples were formed in a water bath at 50&nbsp;°C. Then, the PVDF/VS composite films were fully dried in an oven at 50&nbsp;°C for 24&nbsp;h.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202407783-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202407783-sec-0130-title"> Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <div class="paragraph-element">Morphological and structural analyses were performed by scanning electron microscope (SEM, HITACHI S4800) with energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) and field emission transmission electron microscope (FE-TEM, FEI Tecnai F30). All samples were fractured in liquid nitrogen and then sputtered with gold in vacuum, after which the morphologies of the samples were observed using SEM at an electronic accelerating voltage of 30&nbsp;kV. The elemental compositions were examined by an X-ray photoelectron spectrometer (model: 250Xi, from <i>Thermoelectric Corporation, USA</i>). The crystal patterns of composites were analyzed by an X-ray diffractometer (Model: SmartLab 9KW from <i>Hitachi, Japan</i>). Chemical structures were determined by the <i>Fourier</i> infrared spectrometer, Model: Vertex 80, <i>Bruker Co. Germany</i>. The dynamic mechanical and thermal behavior of the prepared composites were examined by a DMTA tester (model: Q-800, <i>TA Instruments Inc., USA</i>). Testing samples with the dimensions of 0.04 mm&nbsp;×&nbsp;7 mm&nbsp;×&nbsp;30&nbsp;mm were heated to 150&nbsp;°C at a rate of 5&nbsp;°C&nbsp;min<sup>−1</sup>, with modulus curves simultaneously recorded. Raman spectra were carried out from 700 to 1500 cm<sup>−1</sup> (LabRAM HR Evolution). An electronic universal material testing machine (model: Instron-5967, <i>Instron Inc., USA</i>) was used to measure the mechanical properties of the films and commercial separator (CS). The samples were cut into dumbbell shape (preparation was according to ISO 527-2), and testing was carried out (at the ambient temperature) with the actual thickness of the composite films (ca. 20&nbsp;µm) at a constant tensile rate of 10&nbsp;mm&nbsp;min<sup>−1</sup>. Measurement of porosity took the following steps: first, samples were weighed (recorded as <i>m</i><sub>d</sub>) after drying. Then, the samples were completely soaked in ethanol and weighed (recorded as <i>m</i><sub>w</sub>). The calculation formula is shown in Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202407783-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>).

                     <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202407783-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->

                                                <mi>w</mi>
                                             </msub>
                                          </mfrac>
                                          <mo>+</mo>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi>m</mi>
                                             <mi>d</mi>
                                          </msub>
                                          <mo>/</mo>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi>ρ</mi>
                                             <mi>p</mi>
                                          </msub>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </mfrac>
                                 </mrow>
                                 <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}\varepsilon = \frac{{\left( {{{m}_w} - {{m}_d}} \right)/{{\rho }_w}}}{{\frac{{{{m}_w} - {{m}_d}}}{{{{\rho }_w}}} + {{m}_d}/{{\rho }_p}}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                              </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>where <i>m</i><sub>w</sub> and <i>m</i><sub>d</sub> represented the weight of the film before and after immersion, respectively. Parameters <i>ρ</i><sub>w</sub> and <i>ρ</i><sub>p</sub> represented the densities of ethanol and polymer, respectively, with <i>ρ</i><sub>w</sub> = 0.79&nbsp;g&nbsp;cm<sup>−3</sup> in this work. The electrolyte uptake rate was measured as follows: wafers were cut from the sample with a diameter of 16.0&nbsp;mm, and then weighed after drying for 12&nbsp;h (denoted as <i>m</i><sub>1</sub>). The wafers were immersed in the electrolyte for 6&nbsp;h, and then filter papers were used to wipe the electrolyte off the surface. Finally, the wafer was weighed again (denoted as <i>m</i><sub>2</sub>). The calculation formula is shown in Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202407783-disp-0002" title="Link to equation">2</a>).

                     <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202407783-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                           <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/c039431f-5276-42f5-bc9f-d80811ba8aa4/adfm202407783-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202407783:adfm202407783-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202407783-math-0002.png">
                              <semantics>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mi>EU</mi>
                                    <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                                    <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi>
                                          <mn>2</mn>
                                          <mo>−</mo>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi>
                                          <mn>1</mn>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </mfenced>
                                    <mo>/</mo>
                                    <mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi>
                                    <mn>1</mn>
                                    <mo linebreak="goodbreak">×</mo>
                                    <mn>100</mn>
                                    <mo>%</mo>
                                 </mrow>
                                 <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\mathrm{EU}} = \left( {{\mathrm{m}}2 - {\mathrm{m}}1} \right)/{\mathrm{m}}1 \times 100\% \end{equation}$$</annotation>
                              </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
                  </div>
                  
                  <div class="paragraph-element">The films were cut into round wafers (with a diameter of 16.0&nbsp;mm), which were assembled into a gasket/diaphragm/gasket half-cell, followed by AC impedance tests. The formula for calculating the ionic conductivity was given in Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202407783-disp-0003" title="Link to equation">3</a>).

                     <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202407783-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                           <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/9f5abb2d-3809-429e-a492-4b42319b2ef1/adfm202407783-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202407783:adfm202407783-math-0003" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202407783-math-0003.png">
                              <semantics>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mi>σ</mi>
                                    <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                                    <mi mathvariant="normal">d</mi>
                                    <mo>/</mo>
                                    <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi>Rb</mi>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </mfenced>
                                 </mrow>
                                 <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{{\sigma}} = {\mathrm{d}}/\left( {{\mathrm{Rb A}}} \right)\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                              </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>where <i>σ</i> is ionic conductivity, <i>d</i> the thickness of the films, <i>R</i><sub>b</sub> the body resistance of the films, and <i>A</i> the surface area of the films.</div>
                  
                  <p>Thermal shrinkage was evaluated after storing the relevant films in an oven at 200&nbsp;°C for 1&nbsp;h. The thermal stable tests were carried out using forward-looking infrared radiometer (FLIR T1050sc, FLIR Systems, Inc., USA). The hydrophilicity of the PVDF/PEI composite films was evaluated using a contact angle tester (DSA 30, KRUSS., Germany). The SEI component was collected by time-off light secondary ion mass spectrometry (TOF-SIMS, PHI nanoTOF II, 30 keV, 2 nA) in a 200 µm (length) × 200  µm (width) ×100 nm (thickness) region. The roughness and Young's modulus of the SEI were measured by AFM (Bruker Dimension Ico).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202407783-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202407783-sec-0140-title"> Electrochemical Performances</h4>
                  
                  <p>Ac impedance, linear scanning voltammetry (LSV) on separators and Nyquist plots were carried out using an electrochemical workstation (CHI-660e, CH Instruments Inc.). A cell tester (model: BTS-51, <i>Neware Co., China</i>) was used to investigate the effect of the prepared separators on NMBs performance, with NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> used as the positive active substance. The slurry (with an 8:1:1 mass ratio of cathode conductive carbon black and PVDF, respectively) was evenly coated on the special aluminum foil for the coin battery. The active material content of each positive plate was ≈5.0&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. Then, PVDF/VS composite film was stamped into a 16.0&nbsp;mm diameter wafer and soaked in electrolyte for 1 day. Then the separator was vigorously wiped with blotting paper to remove the free electrolyte. The electrolyte taken from the separator was ≈8.5&nbsp;µg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. Then, they were assembled into a complete battery in an argon glove box, denoted as Na/composite film/ NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cell. After the package, the cell was left to stand for 12&nbsp;h to be stabilized. Subsequently, the charge/discharge and rate performance tests were carried out under the following conditions (constant current rate: 0.2–20 C, voltage: 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V). The pouch cell owns 9 positive plates (with size of 5&nbsp;cm × 8&nbsp;cm), 10 negative plates (5.2&nbsp;cm × 8.2&nbsp;cm), and 10 separators (5.4&nbsp;cm × 8.4&nbsp;cm). The active material area loading of the cathode on one side was ≈15 and 30&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup> for two sides. The pressure in the rolling process of the cathode was set at 20&nbsp;MPa. The sodium metal was mixed with copper foam (90% porosity with 1&nbsp;mm thickness) by melt impregnation at 150&nbsp;°C. After cooled to room temperature, the copper foam was rolled until the thickness of 20&nbsp;µm, in which the sodium metal area loading was ≈7.2&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. The mass of added electrolyte was ≈3&nbsp;g. The total mass of the pouch cell was ≈22&nbsp;g.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202407783-sec-0150">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202407783-sec-0150-title"> Calculation Details</h4>
                  
                  <p>The calculation of the total energy of the density flood was implemented using the first principles calculation package (Vienna ab-initio simulation package (VASP)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202407783-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202407783-bib-0039_R_d25777427e1625" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Comput. Mater. Sci.">39</a>, <a href="#adfm202407783-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202407783-bib-0040_R_d25777427e1625" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Phy. Rev. B">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The phase-five interaction potential between the electron and ion realities was described by the projected affixed wave (PAW).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202407783-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202407783-bib-0041_R_d25777427e1630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Phy. Rev. B">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The exchange-correlation potential was chosen in the form of a local density approximation LDA. The truncation energy of the plane wave was 500&nbsp;eV. the Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>3</sub> structures are sectioned along the (0 1 0) and (0 0 1) planes, respectively, and 2 × 2 supercells are used to simulate the migration paths of Na on the Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>3</sub> planes after sectioning. A k-point grid with Brillouin zone integrals 1 × 1 × 2 was also adopted,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202407783-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202407783-bib-0042_R_d25777427e1653" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Phy. Rev. B">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> with a convergence criterion of 10<sup>−6</sup>&nbsp;eV for energy and 0.02&nbsp;eV&nbsp;Å<sup>−1</sup> for force.</p>
                  
                  <p>In addition, the Cl-NEB method was employed to study the kinetics of sodium ion migration. First, the initial and end-state structures of Na on the surfaces of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>3</sub> were optimized to ensure that the initial and end-state structures were fully relaxed. Then a linear interpolation between the optimized initial and final state structures was used to search for the minimum reaction activation energy by inserting four intermediate states in the reactants and products.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
